</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Basis of Presentation</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In the opinion of management, the unaudited
interim consolidated condensed financial statements of Applied
Materials, Inc. and its subsidiaries (Applied) included herein
have been prepared on a basis consistent with the
October&nbsp;26, 2003 audited consolidated financial statements
and include all material adjustments, consisting of normal
recurring adjustments, necessary to fairly present the
information set forth therein. These unaudited interim
consolidated condensed financial statements should be read in
conjunction with the audited consolidated financial statements
and the accompanying notes thereto included in Applied&#146;s
Form&nbsp;10-K for the fiscal year ended October&nbsp;26, 2003.
Applied&#146;s results of operations for the three and nine
months ended August&nbsp;1, 2004 are not necessarily indicative
of future operating results.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The preparation of financial statements in
conformity with accounting principles generally accepted in the
United States of America (United States) requires management to
make judgments, estimates and assumptions that affect the
amounts reported in the financial statements and accompanying
notes. Actual results could differ materially from those
estimates.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s fiscal year ends on the last
Sunday in October of each year. Fiscal 2004 contains
53&nbsp;weeks, whereas fiscal 2003 contained 52&nbsp;weeks. The
first fiscal quarter of 2004 contained 14&nbsp;weeks, whereas
the first fiscal quarter of 2003 contained 13&nbsp;weeks.
Accordingly, the first nine months of fiscal 2004 contained
40&nbsp;weeks, whereas the first nine months of fiscal 2003
contained 39&nbsp;weeks.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Certain prior year amounts have been reclassified
to conform to the fiscal 2004 financial statement presentation.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Stock-Based Compensation</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied measures compensation expense for its
stock-based employee compensation plans using the intrinsic
value method. As the exercise price of all options granted under
these plans was equal to the fair market price of the underlying
common stock on the grant date, no stock-based employee
compensation expense was recognized in the consolidated
condensed statements of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In accordance with Statement of Financial
Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;148, &#147;Accounting for
Stock-Based Compensation&nbsp;&#151; Transition and
Disclosure&nbsp;&#151; an Amendment of Financial Accounting
Standards Board (FASB) Statement No.&nbsp;123&#148;
(SFAS&nbsp;148) and Statement of Financial Accounting Standards
No.&nbsp;123, &#147;Accounting for Stock-Based
Compensation&#148; (SFAS&nbsp;123), Applied&#146;s pro forma
option expense is computed using the Black-Scholes option
pricing model. This model was developed for use in estimating
the value of publicly traded options that have no vesting
restrictions and are fully transferable. Applied&#146;s employee
stock options have characteristics significantly different from
those of traded options; therefore, in the opinion of
management, the Black-Scholes option pricing model generally
used to comply with SFAS&nbsp;148 and SFAS&nbsp;123 does not
necessarily provide a reliable measure of the fair value of
Applied&#146;s options.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">To comply with SFAS&nbsp;148, Applied is
presenting the following table to illustrate the effect on the
net income/ (loss) and earnings/ (loss) per share if it had
applied the fair value recognition provisions of SFAS&nbsp;123,
as amended, to options granted under the stock-based employee
compensation plans. For purposes of this pro forma disclosure,
the estimated value of the options is recognized over the
options&#146; vesting periods.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Based on the Black-Scholes option pricing model,
the weighted average estimated fair value of employee stock
option grants was $7.41 and $8.91 for the three months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004, respectively, and
was $6.80 and $10.34 for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004, respectively. The weighted average
estimated fair value of purchase rights under employee stock
purchase plans (ESPP) was $5.58 and $5.94 for the three months
ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004, respectively.
For the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1,
2004, the weighted average estimated fair value of purchase
rights under ESPP was $5.24 and $5.90, respectively. For
purposes of this calculation, the fair value of each stock
option grant and stock purchase right is estimated on the date
of grant using the Black-Scholes option pricing model and the
following assumptions:

<TR valign="top">
    <TD><B><FONT size="2">2)</FONT></B></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><FONT size="2">Earnings/(Loss) Per Share</FONT></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Basic earnings/ (loss) per share has been
determined using the weighted average number of common shares
outstanding during the period. In the event of a net loss, basic
loss per share is the same as diluted loss per share. Diluted
earnings per share has been determined using the weighted
average number of common shares and equivalents (representing
the dilutive effect of stock options) outstanding during the
period.
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">6
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has agreements with various financial
institutions to sell accounts receivable from selected
customers. Applied also discounts letters of credit through
various financial institutions. Under these agreements, Applied
sold accounts receivable and discounted letters of credit in the
amounts of $130&nbsp;million and $377&nbsp;million for the three
months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004,
respectively, and of $424&nbsp;million and $729&nbsp;million for
the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1,
2004, respectively. Discounting fees were not material for all
periods presented. As of August&nbsp;1, 2004, $35&nbsp;million
of sold accounts receivable remained outstanding under these
agreements. A portion of these sold accounts receivable is
subject to certain recourse provisions. Applied has not
experienced any losses under these recourse provisions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

market, with cost determined on a first-in, first-out (FIFO)
basis. Components of inventories were as follows:
</FONT>

<CENTER>
<TABLE width="80%" align="center" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" border="0">

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In connection with the adoption of Statement of
Financial Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;142, &#147;Goodwill and
Other Intangible Assets&#148; (SFAS&nbsp;142), as of the
beginning of fiscal 2002, goodwill is no longer amortized and is
reviewed annually during the fourth fiscal quarter for
impairment and whenever events and changes in circumstances
suggest that the goodwill carrying amount may not be
recoverable. From October&nbsp;27, 2003 to August&nbsp;1, 2004,
the change in goodwill was approximately $26&nbsp;million, which
consisted of $11&nbsp;million for the acquisition of Torrex
Equipment Corporation (Torrex) completed in the third fiscal
quarter of 2004 (see Note&nbsp;11) with the remainder associated
with adjustments to the purchase price for prior acquisitions
containing contingent purchase price provisions.
</FONT>

<FONT size="2">Gross purchased technology increased by
$8&nbsp;million due to purchased technology acquired during the
first nine months of 2004. Purchased technology and other
intangible assets are amortized over their estimated useful
lives of five to 10&nbsp;years using the straight-line method.
Aggregate amortization expense was $12&nbsp;million and
$13&nbsp;million for the three months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003
and August&nbsp;1, 2004, respectively, and $36&nbsp;million and
$38&nbsp;million for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003
and August&nbsp;1, 2004, respectively. As of August&nbsp;1,
2004, future estimated amortization expense is expected to be
$12&nbsp;million for the remainder of fiscal 2004,
$21&nbsp;million for fiscal 2005, $15&nbsp;million for fiscal
2006, $7&nbsp;million for fiscal 2007, $4&nbsp;million for
fiscal 2008 and $3&nbsp;million thereafter.

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied products are generally sold with a
12-month warranty period following installation. Parts and labor
are covered under the terms of the warranty agreement. The
warranty provision is based on historical experience by product,
configuration and geographic region. Quarterly warranty
consumption is associated with sales that occurred during the
preceding four quarters, and quarterly warranty provisions are
generally related to the current quarter&#146;s sales.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the ordinary course of business, Applied
also provides standby letters of credit or other guarantee
agreements to certain parties as required for certain
transactions initiated by either Applied or its subsidiaries. As
of August&nbsp;1, 2004, the maximum potential amount of future
payments that Applied could be required to make under these
guarantee agreements was approximately $49&nbsp;million. Applied
has not recorded any liability in connection with these
guarantee agreements beyond that required to appropriately
account for the underlying transaction being guaranteed. Applied
does not believe, based on historical experience and information
currently available, that it is probable that any amounts will
be required to be paid under these guarantee agreements.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied also has additional guarantee agreements
on behalf of certain subsidiaries. As of August&nbsp;1, 2004,
Applied has not recorded any liability related to guarantees of
subsidiary obligations. Applied does not expect, based on
historical experience and information currently available, that
it is probable any amounts will be required to be paid under
these agreements. Subsidiary guarantees as of August&nbsp;1,
2004 were associated with the following types of agreements:
short-term borrowings, overdrafts and leases. While certain
subsidiaries had short-term borrowings and overdraft facilities
available totaling approximately $192&nbsp;million as of
August&nbsp;1, 2004, no amounts were outstanding as of the end
of the third fiscal quarter of 2004. In the event of use and
subsequent default of these facilities by Applied&#146;s
subsidiaries, such agreements would be guaranteed by
</FONT>

</FONT>

<P align="left">
<FONT size="2">Applied. In addition, certain subsidiaries have
lease agreements guaranteed by Applied. These leases will expire
between 2009 and 2014. In the event that the subsidiaries do not
make the required payments, Applied could be required to pay the
leases on behalf of the subsidiaries. As of August&nbsp;1, 2004,
quarterly lease obligations under these agreements approximated
$3&nbsp;million.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In connection with certain business combinations
and purchased technology acquisitions, Applied was subject to
contingent consideration agreements at August&nbsp;1, 2004.
These agreements are based upon sales volume or other events
taking place subsequent to the acquisition. Depending upon the
circumstances of each agreement, the payment of the contingency
will result in an increase to goodwill or a charge to operating
expenses. Amounts paid under these agreements have not had, and
are not expected to have, a material effect on Applied&#146;s
financial condition or results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied is subject to various legal proceedings
and claims, either asserted or unasserted, that arise in the
ordinary course of business. Applied from time to time is, and
in the future may be, involved in legal proceedings or claims
regarding patent infringement, intellectual property rights,
antitrust, environmental regulations, securities, contracts,
product performance, product liability, employment and other
matters. In addition, Applied on occasion receives notification
from customers who believe that Applied owes them
indemnification or other obligations related to infringement
claims made against the customers by third parties. Applied
evaluates, among other factors, the degree of probability of an
unfavorable outcome and reasonably estimates the amount of the
loss. Significant judgment is required in evaluating these
factors. When Applied determines that a loss is probable, and
the amount of the loss is reasonably estimatible, the effect is
recorded in the consolidated financial statements. Significant
changes in legal proceedings and claims or in the factors
considered in the evaluation of those matters could have a
material adverse effect on Applied&#146;s business, financial
condition and results of operations. Discussion of legal matters
is incorporated by reference from Part&nbsp;II, Item&nbsp;1,
Legal Proceedings, of this report, and should be considered as
an integral part of the Consolidated Condensed Financial
Statements and the accompanying Notes.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">7)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Restructuring,
Asset Impairments and Other Charges</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In response to difficult business conditions,
Applied implemented a series of realignment activities during
fiscal year 2003. These actions were intended to better align
Applied&#146;s cost structure with prevailing economic
conditions. These realignment activities were completed in the
first fiscal quarter of 2004.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges for the first fiscal quarter of 2004 totaled
$167&nbsp;million, consisting of $65&nbsp;million for facilities
consolidations, $96&nbsp;million for other costs and
$6&nbsp;million for severance and benefits. The $96&nbsp;million
of other costs primarily consisted of $102&nbsp;million in fixed
asset write-offs associated with facility consolidations during
the first fiscal quarter of 2004, which was partially offset by
a $6&nbsp;million reversal of restructuring reserves associated
with prior periods&#146; restructuring plans. In connection with
the restructuring action in the first fiscal quarter of 2004,
Applied consolidated certain facilities located primarily in
Santa&nbsp;Clara, California. Additionally, Applied reduced its
global workforce by approximately 130&nbsp;positions or one
percent. The majority of the affected employees and facilities
were based in Santa&nbsp;Clara, California and Europe and
represented multiple company functions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges were $66&nbsp;million for the third fiscal quarter of
2003. As part of this restructuring, Applied reduced its
infrastructure and global workforce by approximately
450&nbsp;positions or three percent. The majority of the
affected employees and facilities were based in North America,
Asia and Europe and represented multiple company functions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges were $93&nbsp;million for the second fiscal quarter of
2003. As part of this restructuring, Applied reduced its
infrastructure and global workforce by approximately
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">10
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<FONT size="2">1,400&nbsp;positions or 10&nbsp;percent. The
majority of the affected employees and facilities were based in
Santa&nbsp;Clara, California and Austin, Texas and represented
multiple company functions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other

global workforce by approximately 1,750 positions or
11&nbsp;percent. The majority of the affected employees and

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The majority of other restructuring reserves are
expected to be utilized by the end of fiscal 2004, while the
restructuring reserves associated with facilities are expected
to be utilized for lease obligations that continue through
fiscal 2009.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">8)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Derivative
Financial Instruments</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s derivative financial instruments,
consisting of forward exchange and option contracts, are
recorded at fair value on the balance sheet, either in other
current assets or accounts payable and accrued expenses. Changes
in the fair value of derivatives that do not qualify for hedge
treatment, as well as the ineffective portion of any hedges, are
recognized in results of operations. The effective portion of
the gain/(loss) is reported as a component of accumulated other
comprehensive income in stockholders&#146; equity, and is
reclassified into results of operations when the hedged
transaction affects income/(loss). All amounts included in
accumulated other comprehensive income as of August&nbsp;1, 2004
will be reclassified to results of operations within
12&nbsp;months. Changes in the fair value of currency option
contracts due to changes in time value are excluded from the
assessment of effectiveness, and are recognized in cost of
products sold. The change in option time value was not material
for all periods presented. If the transaction being hedged fails
to occur, or if a portion of any derivative is deemed to be
ineffective, Applied recognizes the gain/(loss) on the
associated financial instrument in general and administrative
expenses. The amounts recognized due to the anticipated
transactions failing to occur were not material for all periods
presented.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Accumulated other comprehensive income related to
derivative activities for the nine months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004 increased by $3&nbsp;million, which was comprised of a
$8&nbsp;million net increase in the fair value of derivatives,
partially offset by $5&nbsp;million of net realized gains which
were reclassified from accumulated other comprehensive income to
results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">11
</FONT>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Stock Repurchase Program</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Since March 1996, Applied has systematically
repurchased shares of its common stock to partially fund its
stock-based employee benefit plans. Upon the expiration of the
prior share repurchase authorization on March&nbsp;24, 2004, the
Board of Directors extended the stock repurchase program and
authorized the repurchase of up to $3.0&nbsp;billion of
Applied&#146;s common stock in the open market over the
succeeding three years. Under this authorization, Applied has a
systematic stock repurchase program and may also make additional
stock repurchases from time to time, both of which are dependent
on market conditions, stock price and other factors.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the three months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003,
Applied repurchased 4,951,000&nbsp;shares of its common stock at
an average price of $15.15 for a total cash outlay of
$75&nbsp;million. During the three months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004, Applied did not repurchase any stock as a result of
potential transactions under consideration. During the nine
months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004, Applied
repurchased 12,019,000&nbsp;shares of its common stock at an
average price of $14.55, for a total cash outlay of
$175&nbsp;million and 6,791,000&nbsp;shares of its common stock
at an average price of $22.09 for a total cash outlay of
$150&nbsp;million, respectively.
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">12
</FONT>

<B><FONT size="2">APPLIED MATERIALS, INC.</FONT></B>

<B><FONT size="2">NOTES TO CONSOLIDATED CONDENSED FINANCIAL
STATEMENTS&nbsp;&#151;&nbsp;(Continued)</FONT></B>

<DIV align="center">
<B><FONT size="2">(Unaudited)</FONT></B>
</DIV>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">10)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Notes
Payable</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has credit facilities for unsecured
borrowings in various currencies up to approximately
$673&nbsp;million, of which $500&nbsp;million is comprised of
two revolving credit agreements in the United States with a
group of banks. One agreement is a $250&nbsp;million line of
credit that expires in September 2004 and is expected to be
renewed, and the other is a $250&nbsp;million line of credit
that expires in September 2006. The agreements provide for
borrowings at various rates, including the lead bank&#146;s
prime reference rate, and include financial and other covenants
with which Applied was in compliance as of October&nbsp;26, 2003
and August&nbsp;1, 2004. No amounts were outstanding under these
agreements as of October&nbsp;26, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004.
The remaining credit facilities of approximately
$173&nbsp;million are with Japanese banks at rates indexed to
their prime reference rate and are denominated in Japanese yen.
No amounts were outstanding under these Japanese credit
facilities as of October&nbsp;26, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">11)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Business
Combinations</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On June&nbsp;14, 2004, Applied acquired Torrex, a
developer of a multi-wafer processor that utilizes chemical
vapor deposition and atomic layer deposition processes to
address front-end semiconductor manufacturing applications, for
$7&nbsp;million in cash. In connection with this acquisition,
Applied recorded goodwill of $11&nbsp;million, net of
adjustments to the initial purchase price allocation, partially
offset by other items of $4&nbsp;million, primarily for net
liabilities assumed upon acquisition. The in-process research
and development expense was not material. The results of
operations for Torrex prior to the acquisition date were not
material in relation to those of Applied for any of the periods
presented herein.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On April&nbsp;18, 2003, Applied acquired Boxer
Cross, Inc., a producer of in-line monitoring systems that
provide customers with critical electrical measurement data for
controlling semiconductor processes, for $14&nbsp;million in
cash. In connection with this acquisition, Applied recorded
goodwill of $18&nbsp;million, net of adjustments to the initial
purchase price allocation, and purchased technology of
$3&nbsp;million, partially offset by other items of
$7&nbsp;million, primarily for net liabilities assumed upon
acquisition. The in-process research and development expense was
not material. The results of operations for Boxer Cross, Inc.
prior to the acquisition date were not material in relation to
those of Applied for any of the periods presented herein.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">12)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consolidation
of Variable Interest Entities</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has a venture capital fund, Applied
Materials Ventures&nbsp;I, L.P. (the Fund), that invests in
privately-held, early-stage companies engaged in developing
systems, components and devices relating to nanotechnology
and/or communications technology for specific applications and
products. The Fund is a limited partnership with Applied as the
sole limited partner and an independent party as the general
partner. Applied has committed to fund $50&nbsp;million in
capital contributions, but has reserved the option to
discontinue capital contributions at $25&nbsp;million.
Applied&#146;s capital contributions to the Fund totaled
approximately $16&nbsp;million through October&nbsp;26, 2003 and
$19&nbsp;million through August&nbsp;1, 2004.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">FASB Interpretation No.&nbsp;46,
&#147;Consolidation of Variable Interest Entities, an
Interpretation of ARB No.&nbsp;51&#148; (FIN&nbsp;46), as
amended, provides guidance on the identification, classification
and accounting of variable interest entities. The Fund qualifies
for consolidation under FIN&nbsp;46 and was consolidated in
Applied&#146;s financial statements during the first fiscal
quarter of 2004. The consolidation of the Fund did not have a
material impact on Applied&#146;s financial condition or results
of operations in the period of implementation of FIN&nbsp;46, as
amended, or for the three or nine months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004.
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">13
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">13)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Recent
Accounting Pronouncements</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In June 2004, the FASB issued Emerging Issues
Task Force Issue No.&nbsp;03-1, &#147;The Meaning of
Other-Than-Temporary Impairment and Its Application to Certain
Investments&#148; (EITF&nbsp;03-1). EITF&nbsp;03-1 includes new
guidance for evaluating and recording impairment losses on debt
and equity investments, as well as new disclosure requirements
for investments that are deemed to be temporarily impaired. The
recognition and measurement guidance of EITF&nbsp;03-1 will be
effective for Applied&#146;s fourth fiscal quarter of 2004. The
Company is in the process of evaluating the effect of adopting
EITF&nbsp;03-1.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On December&nbsp;17, 2003, the Securities and
Exchange Commission (SEC)&nbsp;issued Staff Accounting Bulletin
No.&nbsp;104, &#147;Revenue Recognition&#148; (SAB&nbsp;104),
which superseded Staff Accounting Bulletin No.&nbsp;101,
&#147;Revenue Recognition in Financial Statements&#148;
(SAB&nbsp;101). The revenue recognition principles of
SAB&nbsp;101 remain largely unchanged by the issuance of
SAB&nbsp;104. SAB&nbsp;104 also recognized the existing guidance
on revenue arrangements with multiple deliverables. The adoption
of SAB&nbsp;104 has not had, and is not expected to have, a
material effect on Applied&#146;s financial condition or results
of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">14)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Subsequent
Event</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On August&nbsp;16, 2004, Applied announced that
it had entered into a definitive agreement to acquire the
operating subsidiaries and businesses of Metron Technology N.V.,
which provides a wide range of outsource solutions to the
semiconductor industry, for approximately $85&nbsp;million,
subject to certain closing conditions. The results of operations
for Metron Technology N.V. are not expected to be material in
relation to Applied&#146;s results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Overview</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied develops, manufactures, markets and
services integrated circuit fabrication equipment for the
worldwide semiconductor industry. Applied&#146;s product
offerings consist of systems, components, spare parts, and
services. Demand for Applied&#146;s products can change
significantly from period to period as a result of numerous
factors, including, but not limited to, changes in:
(1)&nbsp;global economic conditions; (2)&nbsp;advanced
technology and/or capacity requirements of semiconductor
manufacturers; (3)&nbsp;the profitability of semiconductor
manufacturers; (4)&nbsp;supply and demand for semiconductors;
and (5)&nbsp;the relative competitiveness of Applied&#146;s
products. For this and other reasons, Applied&#146;s results of
operations for the three and nine months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004 may not be indicative of future operating results.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Beginning with the fourth fiscal quarter of 2003
and continuing into fiscal 2004, customers began making
increased investments in capital equipment for capacity and
technology, responding to higher spending for consumer
electronics and business information technology and increases in
semiconductor demand. This recovery was preceded by an industry
downturn, extending from the first fiscal quarter of 2001
through the third fiscal quarter of 2003. This downturn, which
Applied believes was the most severe downturn in the history of
the semiconductor industry, resulted in a significant decrease
in demand for integrated circuit fabrication equipment, lower
investment in fab capacity, and customers&#146; delays in
placing new orders.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Results of Operations</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s fiscal year ends on the last
Sunday in October of each year. Fiscal 2004 contains
53&nbsp;weeks, whereas fiscal 2003 contained 52&nbsp;weeks. The
first fiscal quarter of 2004 contained 14&nbsp;weeks, whereas
the first fiscal quarter of 2003 contained 13&nbsp;weeks.
Accordingly, the first nine months of fiscal 2004 contained
40&nbsp;weeks, whereas the first nine months of fiscal 2003
contained 39&nbsp;weeks.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied received new orders of $2.5&nbsp;billion
for the third fiscal quarter of 2004, compared to
$2.2&nbsp;billion for the second fiscal quarter of 2004 and
$1.1&nbsp;billion for the third fiscal quarter of 2003. New
orders for the third fiscal quarter of 2004 increased by
11&nbsp;percent from the preceding quarter and by
134&nbsp;percent from the third fiscal quarter of 2003. The
increase in orders reflected a broad-based increase in capital
investment by both DRAM and logic manufacturers for 300mm
technology, as well as capital investment by customers for
capacity expansion for 200mm technology to meet rising demand
for semiconductors.
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">15

<TR>

    <TD width="97%"></TD>

    <TD><FONT size="2">**&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
    <TD align="left">
    <FONT size="2">Includes China
    </FONT></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As indicated in the table above, the order growth
in the third fiscal quarter of 2004 was centered in Taiwan,
Europe, Japan and North America, reflecting increased capital
investment for both 300mm technology and capacity expansion.
Orders for Asia-Pacific in the third fiscal quarter were lower
than the prior quarter due to a large order received in the
second fiscal quarter that did not recur in the third fiscal
quarter.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s backlog for the most recent three
fiscal quarters was as follows: $3.0&nbsp;billion as of
August&nbsp;1, 2004, $2.8&nbsp;billion as of May&nbsp;2, 2004
and $2.6&nbsp;billion as of February&nbsp;1, 2004. Backlog
consists only of orders for which written authorizations have
been accepted, shipment dates within 12&nbsp;months have been
assigned and revenue has not been recognized. Due to the
potential for customer changes in delivery schedules or
cancellation of orders, Applied&#146;s backlog at any particular
time is not necessarily indicative of actual sales for any
future periods.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Net sales continued to increase from
$1.2&nbsp;billion for the fourth fiscal quarter of 2003,
$1.6&nbsp;billion for the first fiscal quarter of 2004,
$2.0&nbsp;billion for the second fiscal quarter of 2004 to
$2.2&nbsp;billion for the third fiscal quarter of 2004, after
having been relatively flat at $1.1&nbsp;billion for each of the
first three fiscal quarters of 2003. Net sales for the third
fiscal quarter of 2004 increased 11&nbsp;percent from the second
fiscal quarter of 2004 and 104&nbsp;percent from the third
fiscal quarter of 2003. The overall increase in net sales was
primarily due to the fulfillment of higher levels of orders for
capital equipment received in prior quarters to support
customers&#146; expansion of capacity and technology.
</FONT>

ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004 were as follows:

<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As indicated in the table above, net sales for
the third fiscal quarter of 2004 increased year over year for
all regions, reflecting an overall improvement in the worldwide
demand for integrated circuit fabrication equipment. Net sales
for the nine months ended August&nbsp;1, 2004 increased in all
regions with the exception of a slight decline in North America
over the comparable period in fiscal 2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Gross margin was 47.4&nbsp;percent for the third
fiscal quarter of 2004, compared to 46.5&nbsp;percent for the
second fiscal quarter of 2004, and 31.7&nbsp;percent for the
third fiscal quarter of 2003. Gross margin was 46.0&nbsp;percent
for the nine months ended August&nbsp;1, 2004, compared to
34.1&nbsp;percent for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003.
The increase in the gross margin percentage from the second
fiscal quarter to the third fiscal quarter of 2004 was
principally attributable to improved revenue levels, decreased
product costs, and increased manufacturing volume. The increase
in gross margin from the three and nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003 to the same periods ended August&nbsp;1, 2004
was due to all the factors discussed above in addition to
charges to cost of products sold for inventory write-offs as a
result of the implementation of the global spares distribution
system and refocused product efforts in 2003. Charges to cost of
products sold for the global spares distribution system and
refocused product efforts totaled $93&nbsp;million and
$142&nbsp;million for the three and nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Operating expenses included expenses related to
research, development and engineering (RD&#38;E), marketing and
selling (M&#38;S), and general and administrative (G&#38;A).
Expenses related to RD&#38;E, M&#38;S and G&#38;A were
$446&nbsp;million for the third fiscal quarter of 2004, compared
to $424&nbsp;million for the second fiscal quarter of 2004 and
$367&nbsp;million for the third fiscal quarter of 2003. For the
nine months ended August&nbsp;1, 2004, these expenses were
$1.3&nbsp;billion, compared to $1.2&nbsp;billion for the same
period in fiscal 2003. The increase in operating expenses was
principally attributable to an increase in variable compensation
costs resulting from the improved financial performance for the
three and nine months ended August&nbsp;1, 2004, partially
offset by a lower cost structure as a result of the
implementation of realignment activities.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In response to difficult business conditions,
Applied implemented a series of realignment activities during
fiscal 2003. These actions were intended to better align
Applied&#146;s cost structure with prevailing economic
conditions. These realignment activities were completed in the
first fiscal quarter of 2004.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges for the first fiscal quarter of 2004 totaled
$167&nbsp;million, consisting of $65&nbsp;million for facilities
consolidations, $96&nbsp;million for other costs and
$6&nbsp;million for severance and benefits. The $96&nbsp;million
of other costs primarily consisted of $102&nbsp;million in fixed
asset write-offs associated with facility consolidations during
the first fiscal quarter of 2004, which was partially offset by
a $6&nbsp;million reversal of fixed asset reserves associated
with prior periods&#146; restructuring plans. In connection with
the restructuring action in the first fiscal quarter of 2004,
Applied consolidated certain facilities located primarily in
Santa&nbsp;Clara, California. Additionally, Applied reduced its
global workforce by approximately 130&nbsp;positions or one
percent. The majority of the affected employees and facilities
were based in Santa&nbsp;Clara, California and Europe and
represented multiple company functions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges were $66&nbsp;million for the third fiscal quarter of
2003. As part of this restructuring, Applied reduced its
infrastructure and global workforce by approximately
450&nbsp;positions or three percent. The majority of the
affected employees and facilities were based in North America,
Asia and Europe and represented multiple company functions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges were $93&nbsp;million for the second fiscal quarter of
2003. As part of this restructuring, Applied reduced its
infrastructure and global workforce by approximately
1,400&nbsp;positions or 10&nbsp;percent. The majority of the
affected employees and facilities were based in
Santa&nbsp;Clara, California and Austin, Texas and represented
multiple company functions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Restructuring, asset impairments and other
charges were $99&nbsp;million for the first fiscal quarter of
2003. In connection with the restructuring action in the first
fiscal quarter of 2003, Applied reduced its infrastructure
</FONT>

</FONT>

<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV align="left">
<FONT size="2">and global workforce by approximately 1,750
positions or 11&nbsp;percent. The majority of the affected
employees and facilities were in Santa&nbsp;Clara, California
and Austin, Texas and represented multiple company functions.
</FONT>
</DIV>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Net interest income, which includes realized
gains/(losses) from the sale of investments, was
$11&nbsp;million and $35&nbsp;million for the three months ended
August&nbsp;1, 2004 and July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively, and
was $45&nbsp;million and $85&nbsp;million for the nine months
ended August&nbsp;1, 2004 and July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively.
Lower net interest income in 2004 was primarily due to lower
average yields and lower realized gains.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s effective income tax provision
rate was 29.5&nbsp;percent for all periods presented.
Applied&#146;s future income tax rate depends on various
factors, such as tax legislation, the geographic composition of
Applied&#146;s pre-tax income, the valuation of Applied&#146;s
deferred tax assets or liabilities, and non-tax deductible
expenses incurred with acquisitions. In addition, Applied is
subject to routine examination of its tax returns by the
Internal Revenue Service and other tax authorities. Applied
regularly assesses the likelihood of adverse outcomes from these
examinations to determine the adequacy of its provision for
income taxes.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Financial Condition, Liquidity and Capital
Resources</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the nine months ended August&nbsp;1, 2004,
Applied increased its cash, cash equivalents and short-term
investments by $1.1&nbsp;billion, from $5.5&nbsp;billion as of
October&nbsp;26, 2003 to $6.6&nbsp;billion as of August&nbsp;1,
2004, reflecting improved operating results and asset management.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied generated $1.1&nbsp;billion of cash from
operating activities for the nine months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004. The primary sources of cash were from net income, an
increase in payables and the utilization of deferred tax assets,
which were partially offset by an increase in accounts
receivable and inventories for the nine months ended
August&nbsp;1, 2004. Applied utilized programs to sell accounts
receivable of $729&nbsp;million for the nine months ended
August&nbsp;1, 2004. These accounts receivable sales had the
effect of increasing cash and reducing accounts receivable. A
portion of these sold accounts receivable is subject to certain
recourse provisions. Applied has not experienced any losses
under these programs. Days sales outstanding in accounts
receivable was 62&nbsp;days at the end of the third fiscal
quarter of 2004, compared to 63&nbsp;days at the end of the
second fiscal quarter of 2004. For further details regarding
accounts receivable sales, see Note&nbsp;3 of Notes to
Consolidated Condensed Financial Statements. Inventories
increased by $202&nbsp;million during the nine months ended
August&nbsp;1, 2004, due to a $256&nbsp;million increase in raw
materials, work-in-process and finished goods inventories to
support increased business volume, which was partially offset by
a $54&nbsp;million decrease in customer service spares inventory.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied used $577&nbsp;million of cash for
investing activities during the nine months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004. Capital expenditures totaled $150&nbsp;million. Applied
utilized $420&nbsp;million in cash for purchases of short-term
investments, net of proceeds from sales and maturities of
short-term investments.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied generated $138&nbsp;million of cash from
financing activities for the nine months ended August&nbsp;1,
2004, primarily consisting of $291&nbsp;million of proceeds from
the issuance of common stock under employee stock option and
employee stock purchase plans, partially offset by
$150&nbsp;million of stock repurchases in the open market.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied requires substantial cash balances to
preserve the financial flexibility it needs to respond
effectively to the industry&#146;s technological transitions,
cyclicality and other unique characteristics. This liquidity
allows Applied to:&nbsp;(1)&nbsp;fund its global infrastructure
and manufacturing and service activities; (2)&nbsp;continue its
extensive RD&#38;E programs; and (3)&nbsp;support other
strategic objectives, including business acquisitions and
repurchases of Applied&#146;s common stock.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Although cash requirements will fluctuate based
on the timing and extent of many factors, Applied&#146;s
management believes that cash generated from operations,
together with the liquidity provided by existing cash balances
and borrowing capability, will be sufficient to satisfy
Applied&#146;s liquidity requirements for the next
12&nbsp;months. For further details regarding Applied&#146;s
operating, investing and financing activities, see the
Consolidated Condensed Statements of Cash Flows.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The preparation of financial statements and
related disclosures in conformity with accounting principles
generally accepted in the United States of America requires
management to make judgments, assumptions and estimates that
affect the amounts reported. Please see the discussion of
critical accounting policies in Applied&#146;s Annual Report on
Form&nbsp;10-K for the fiscal year ended October&nbsp;26, 2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Trends, Risks and Uncertainties</FONT></B>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">The industry that Applied serves is highly
    volatile and unpredictable.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As a supplier to the global semiconductor
industry, Applied is subject to the industry&#146;s business
cycles, the timing, length and volatility of which are difficult
to predict. The semiconductor industry has historically been
cyclical due to sudden changes in demand for semiconductors and
manufacturing capacity, including capacity using the latest
technology. The rate of changes in demand, including
end-customer demand, is accelerating, and the effect of these
changes on Applied is occurring sooner, exacerbating the
volatility of these cycles. These changes have affected the
timing and amounts of customers&#146; capital equipment
purchases and investments in technology, and continue to affect
Applied&#146;s orders, net sales, gross margin and results of
operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During periods of increasing demand for
integrated circuit manufacturing equipment, Applied must have
sufficient manufacturing capacity and inventory to meet customer
demand, and must be able to attract, retain and motivate a
sufficient number of qualified individuals. If Applied is unable
to effectively manage its resources and production capacity
during an industry upturn, there could be a material adverse
effect on its business, financial condition and results of
operations. Conversely, in downturns, Applied must be able to
appropriately align its cost structure with prevailing market
conditions and effectively motivate and retain key employees. If
Applied is not able to timely align its cost structure with
business conditions, Applied&#146;s business, financial
condition or results of operations may be materially and
adversely affected.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to the risks of
    operating a global business.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the third fiscal quarter of 2004, more
than 80&nbsp;percent of Applied&#146;s net sales were to regions
outside the United States. Certain manufacturing facilities and
suppliers of Applied are also located outside the United States.
Managing Applied&#146;s global operations presents challenges
including, but not limited to, those arising from periodic
regional economic downturns, global trade issues, varying
business conditions and demands, variations in enforcement of
intellectual property and other legal rights in different
jurisdictions, differences in the ability to develop
relationships with suppliers and other local businesses, changes
in United States and international laws and regulations,
including United States export restrictions, fluctuations in
interest and currency exchange rates, the need to provide
sufficient levels of technical support in different locations,
cultural differences and shipping delays. Many of these
challenges are present in China, a large potential market for
integrated circuit equipment and an area that Applied
anticipates will continue to present a significant opportunity
for growth. These challenges, as well as global uncertainties
with respect to: (1)&nbsp;economic growth rates in various
countries; (2)&nbsp;consumer confidence;
(3)&nbsp;sustainability, timing, rate and amount of demand for
electronics products and semiconductors; (4)&nbsp;capital
spending by semiconductor manufacturers; (5)&nbsp;price trends
for certain semiconductor devices; and (6)&nbsp;political
instability, terrorism or acts of war where Applied has
operations or sales, including Asia, Israel and the United
States, may materially and adversely affect Applied&#146;s
business, financial condition and results of operations.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to risks as a result of
    ongoing changes in the semiconductor industry.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The semiconductor industry is characterized by
rapid ongoing changes, including: (1)&nbsp;more complex
technology requirements; (2)&nbsp;the changing information
technology cost structure and the importance of driving down
cost of ownership; (3)&nbsp;the increasing significance of
consumer electronics as a driver for chip demand and the related
focus on lower prices; (4)&nbsp;varying levels of business
information technology spending; (5)&nbsp;the growing types and
varieties of integrated circuits and applications; (6)&nbsp;an
increasing number of applications across multiple substrate
sizes in the semiconductor industry, resulting in divergent
interests
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">19
</FONT>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV align="left">
<FONT size="2">among semiconductor manufacturers;
(7)&nbsp;changes in customers&#146; capacity requirements;
(8)&nbsp;a rising percentage of business from customers in Asia
and emergence of customers and competitors in new geographical
regions; (9)&nbsp;customer demands for shorter lead times for
the manufacture and installation of semiconductor manufacturing
equipment; and (10)&nbsp;higher capital requirements for new
semiconductor fabrication plants. These changes, individually or
in combination, are increasing the need for customer partnering,
use of foundries, collaborative research and development efforts
and process integration support. These changes also heighten the
importance of spare parts and service product offerings as a
competitive advantage for semiconductor equipment manufacturers,
even though service products historically have resulted, and may
in the future result, in lower gross margins than system
products. In addition, key integrated circuit manufacturers have
become influential in technology decisions made by their global
partners. If Applied does not successfully manage the risks
resulting from the ongoing changes occurring in the
semiconductor industry, its business, financial condition and
results of operation could be materially and adversely affected.
</FONT>
</DIV>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied operates in a highly competitive
    industry characterized by increasingly rapid technological
    changes.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As Applied operates in a highly competitive
environment, Applied&#146;s future success is heavily dependent
on effective development, commercialization and customer
acceptance of its new products and services over those of its
competitors. Applied&#146;s success is subject to many risks,
including but not limited to its ability to timely and
cost-effectively: (1)&nbsp;develop and market new products,
services and technologies; (2)&nbsp;improve existing products,
services and technologies; (3)&nbsp;expand into or develop
related and new markets for integrated circuit products and
services; (4)&nbsp;achieve market acceptance and accurately
forecast demand for its products and services;
(5)&nbsp;transition from 200mm systems to 300mm systems while
still maintaining sufficient 200mm technological capabilities;
(6)&nbsp;qualify new or improved products for volume
manufacturing with its customers; (7)&nbsp;commence and adjust
production to meet customer demands; (8)&nbsp;price products and
services appropriately; and (9)&nbsp;lower customers&#146; cost
of ownership. The development, introduction and support of an
increasingly broad set of new or improved products, services and
technologies, including those enabling the transition to smaller
device feature sizes, new materials and 300mm wafers, have grown
increasingly complex and expensive over time. Such new or
improved products and services may involve higher costs and
reduced efficiencies compared to Applied&#146;s more established
products and services and could adversely affect Applied&#146;s
gross margins. If Applied does not develop and introduce new or
improved products, services and technologies in a timely and
cost-effective manner in response to changing market conditions
or customer requirements, and/or if Applied is not able to price
such products and services appropriately to reflect increased
costs, its business, financial condition and results of
operations could be materially and adversely affected.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to risks associated with
    acquisitions.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has made, and may in the future make,
acquisitions of, or significant investments in, businesses with
complementary or aligned products, services and/or technologies.
Acquisitions involve numerous risks, including but not limited
to: (1)&nbsp;diversion of management&#146;s attention from other
operational matters; (2)&nbsp;the inability to complete
acquisitions as anticipated or at all; (3)&nbsp;the inability to
realize synergies expected to result from an acquisition;
(4)&nbsp;failure to commercialize purchased technologies;
(5)&nbsp;impairment of acquired intangible assets as a result of
technological advancements or worse-than-expected performance of
the acquired company or its product offerings;
(6)&nbsp;effectiveness of an acquired company&#146;s internal
controls; (7)&nbsp;integration and retention of key employees;
and (8)&nbsp;integration of operations. Mergers and acquisitions
are inherently subject to significant risks, and the inability
to effectively manage these risks could materially and adversely
affect Applied&#146;s business, financial condition and results
of operations.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to risks associated with
    expanded service product offerings.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In order to improve customers&#146; manufacturing
productivity and efficiency, Applied has entered into, and may
in the future enter into, agreements that expand its service
product offerings beyond those relating to Applied&#146;s
systems. These service products include on-site support as well
as supply chain and spare parts
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">20
</FONT>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#tocpage">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV align="left">
<FONT size="2">management and planning. These new service
products are offered in part through strategic alliances formed
with, or acquisitions of, other suppliers to the semiconductor
industry. In order to develop this market opportunity, Applied
must cultivate new business models, form and maintain strategic
relationships with appropriate companies, achieve customer
acceptance, and successfully and cost-effectively provide these
services. Applied&#146;s inability to achieve any of the
foregoing could have a material adverse effect on Applied&#146;s
business, financial condition and results of operations.
</FONT>
</DIV>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to risks associated with
    a highly concentrated customer base.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s customer base is and has been
highly concentrated. Orders from a relatively limited number of
manufacturers of integrated circuits have accounted for, and
likely will continue to account for, a substantial portion of
Applied&#146;s net sales, which may lead customers to demand
pricing and other terms less favorable to Applied. In addition,
sales to any single customer may vary significantly from quarter
to quarter. If customers do not place orders, reschedule or
cancel orders, Applied may not be able to replace this business.
As Applied&#146;s products are configured to customer
specifications, changing, rescheduling or canceling orders may
result in significant non-recoverable costs. The resulting
fluctuations in the amount of and terms of orders could have a
material adverse effect on Applied&#146;s business, financial
condition and results of operations.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Manufacturing interruptions or delays could
    affect Applied&#146;s ability to meet customer demand, while the
    failure to estimate customer demand accurately could result in
    excess or obsolete inventory.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s business depends on its ability to
supply products and services that meet the rapidly changing
demands of its customers, which depends in part on the timely
delivery of parts, components and subassemblies (collectively,
parts) from suppliers. Some key parts may be obtained only from
a single supplier or a limited group of suppliers. In addition,
Applied outsources certain manufacturing activities. Significant
interruptions of manufacturing operations as a result of the
failure or inability of suppliers to timely deliver quality
parts, outsourcing difficulties, natural disasters (such as
earthquakes or tornadoes), or other causes (such as information
technology or infrastructure failures, regional economic
downturns, political instability, terrorism or acts of war)
could result in delayed product or service deliveries or
manufacturing inefficiencies. Conversely, if actual demand for
Applied&#146;s products and services is less than expected,
Applied may purchase more parts than necessary or incur costs
for canceling or postponing delivery of parts. Any or all of
these factors could materially and adversely affect
Applied&#146;s business, financial condition and results of
operations.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">The ability to attract, retain, and
    motivate key employees is vital to Applied&#146;s
    success.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s success depends in large part on
its ability to attract, retain and motivate key employees,
including those in managerial, technical, marketing and support
roles. Achieving this objective may be difficult due to changes
in the global economy, the industry and Applied&#146;s
management and proposed changes in accounting rules that would
require expensing of stock options. If Applied does not
successfully attract, retain and motivate key employees,
Applied&#146;s operating results and ability to capitalize on
its opportunities may be materially and adversely affected.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

    to legal proceedings or claims and protection of intellectual
    property rights.</FONT></I></B></TD>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied from time to time is, and in the future
may be, involved in legal proceedings or claims regarding patent
infringement, intellectual property rights, antitrust,
environmental regulations, securities, contracts, product
performance, product liability, employment and other matters. In
addition, Applied on occasion receives notification from
customers who believe that Applied owes them indemnification or
other obligations related to infringement claims made against
the customers by third parties. These legal proceedings and
claims, whether with or without merit, are time-consuming and
expensive to prosecute or defend and divert management&#146;s
attention and resources. There can be no assurance regarding the
outcome of current or future legal proceedings or claims. In
addition, Applied&#146;s intellectual property rights may not
provide significant competitive advantages if they are
circumvented, invalidated or rendered obsolete by the rapid pace
of technological change. Furthermore, the laws of other
countries permit the protection of Applied&#146;s rights to
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">21
</FONT>

<FONT size="2">varying extents, compared to United States laws.
Applied&#146;s success is dependent in part upon the protection
of its intellectual property and other rights. Infringement of
Applied&#146;s rights by a third party could result in
uncompensated lost market and revenue opportunities for Applied.
If Applied is not able to resolve a claim, negotiate a
settlement of the matter, obtain necessary licenses on
commercially reasonable terms, and/or successfully prosecute or
defend its position, Applied&#146;s business, financial
condition and results of operations could be materially and

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied is subject to various risks related to:
(1)&nbsp;new, different, inconsistent or even conflicting laws,
rules and regulations that may be enacted by legislative bodies
and/or regulatory agencies in the countries in which Applied
operates and with which Applied must comply; and
(2)&nbsp;disagreements or disputes between national or regional
regulatory agencies related to international trade.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">For example, the World Trade Organization
(WTO)&nbsp;has determined that the United States Foreign Sales
Corporation (FSC)&nbsp;and Extraterritorial Income (ETI)
exclusions constitute prohibited export subsidies. Legislation
currently pending in the United States Congress may eliminate
the FSC and ETI tax provisions but may also contain other
provisions that offset the elimination of these tax benefits.
Applied has benefited from FSC and ETI tax provisions, and the
elimination of these tax provisions without any financially
comparable replacement tax benefits could materially and
adversely affect Applied&#146;s financial condition and results
of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During fiscal 2002, Applied filed an application
with the SEC for an exemptive order confirming that it is not
subject to the Investment Company Act of 1940 (the Act), which
requires companies primarily engaged in the business of
investing in securities to comply with additional rules and
regulations. Based on Applied&#146;s ratios of investments to
total assets and of interest income to net income, Applied could
be deemed to be covered by the Act. In March 2004, Applied
responded to the SEC&#146;s request for additional and updated
information. If the SEC does not grant the exemption, Applied
may have to take other actions that could adversely affect its
results of operations in order not to be subject to the Act.
</FONT>

<!-- link1 "Item 3. Quantitative and Qualitative Disclosures About Market Risk" -->

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="8%"></TD>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD><B><FONT size="2">Item&nbsp;3.</FONT></B></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Quantitative and Qualitative Disclosures
    About Market Risk</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied purchases forward exchange and currency
option contracts to hedge certain existing and anticipated
foreign currency denominated transactions expected to occur
during the next year. Gains/(losses) on these contracts are
generally recognized in income when the related transactions
being hedged are recognized. Net foreign currency gains/(losses)
were not material for the three or nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003 and August&nbsp;1, 2004. Because the effect
of movements in currency exchange rates on forward exchange and
currency option contracts generally offsets the related effect
on the underlying items being hedged, these financial
instruments are not expected to subject Applied to risks that
would otherwise result from changes in currency exchange rates.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s interest income is also exposed to
changes in interest rates.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has performed an analysis to assess the
potential financial effect of reasonably possible near-term
changes in interest and foreign currency exchange rates. Based
upon that analysis, the effect of such rate changes is not
expected to be material to Applied&#146;s financial condition,
results of operations or cash flows.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As required by Rule&nbsp;13a-15(b) of the
Securities Exchange Act of 1934, as amended, Applied management,
including its Chief Executive Officer and Chief Financial
Officer, conducted an evaluation as of the end of the period
covered by this report, of the effectiveness of Applied&#146;s
disclosure controls and procedures as defined in
Rule&nbsp;13a-15(e). Based on that evaluation, the Chief
Executive Officer and Chief Financial Officer concluded that
Applied&#146;s disclosure controls and procedures were effective
as of the end of the period covered by this report. As required
by Rule&nbsp;13a-15(d), Applied management, including its Chief
Executive Officer and Chief Financial Officer, also conducted an
evaluation of Applied&#146;s internal control over financial
reporting to determine whether any changes occurred during the
quarter that have materially affected, or are reasonably likely
to materially affect, Applied&#146;s internal control over
financial reporting. Based on that evaluation, there has been no
such change during the quarter.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">It should be noted that any system of controls,
however well designed and operated, can provide only reasonable,
and not absolute, assurance that the objectives of the system
will be met. In addition, the design of any control system is
based in part upon certain assumptions about the likelihood of
future events.
